HORIZONTAL WELL
P DAM STATE 155-99-4-16-21-13H3
NWNW Section 16-155N-99W
Williams County
Well File # 28539
Dear Mike:
Pursuant to Commission Order No. 23202, approval to drill the above captioned well is hereby given. The approval is
granted on the condition that all portions of the well bore not isolated by cement, be no closer than the 200' setback from the
north & south boundaries and 500' setback from the east & west boundaries within the 1280 acre spacing unit consisting of
Sections 16 & 21 T155N R99W. Tool error is not required pursuant to order.
PERMIT STIPULATIONS: Effective June 1, 2014, a covered leak-proof container (with placard) for filter sock
disposal must be maintained on the well site beginning when the well is spud, and must remain on-site during
clean-out, completion, and flow-back whenever filtration operations are conducted. Due to surficial water adjacent
to the well site, a dike is required surrounding the entire location. SPILL CONTINGENCY (SPCC) PLAN MUST
BE IN PLACE PRIOR TO SPUDDING THE WELL. An onsite inspection is required. Contact NDIC Field
Inspector for site specific stipulations to include but not limited to: Closed Mud System, no drilling pit, and
impermeable liner is required on the entire location and a string of casing must be placed in the rat and mouse
hole and cemented to ground level. Furthermore, the operator must take into consideration the production facility
when placing the liner on location.
Automatic shut offs required on well head and high/low tank indicators.
Blind rams are required on all drilling and workover rigs. KODIAK OIL & GAS (USA) INC must contact NDIC
Field Inspector Jessica Gilkey at 701-770-7340 prior to location construction.
Drilling pit
NDAC 43-02-03-19.4 states that "a pit may be utilized to bury drill cuttings and solids generated during well drilling and
completion operations, providing the pit can be constructed, used and reclaimed in a manner that will prevent pollution of
the land surface and freshwaters. Reserve and circulation of mud system through earthen pits are prohibited. All pits shall be
inspected by an authorized representative of the director prior to lining and use. Drill cuttings and solids must be stabilized
in a manner approved by the director prior to placement in a cuttings pit."
Form 1 Changes & Hard Lines
Any changes, shortening of casing point or lengthening at Total Depth must have prior approval by the NDIC. The
proposed directional plan is at a legal location. Based on the azimuth of the proposed lateral the maximum legal coordinates
from the well head are: 9493 S & 785 W.
Location Construction Commencement (Three Day Waiting Period)
Operators shall not commence operations on a drill site until the 3rd business day following publication of the approved
drilling permit on the NDIC - OGD Daily Activity Report. If circumstances require operations to commence before the 3rd
business day following publication on the Daily Activity Report, the waiting period may be waived by the Director.
Application for a waiver must be by sworn affidavit providing the information necessary to evaluate the extenuating
circumstances, the factors of NDAC 43-02-03-16.2 (1), (a)-(f), and any other information that would allow the Director to
conclude that in the event another owner seeks revocation of the drilling permit, the applicant should retain the permit.
Mike Nash
June 4, 2014
Page 2
Permit Fee & Notification
Payment was received in the amount of $100 via credit card .The permit fee has been received. It is requested that
notification be given immediately upon the spudding of the well. This information should be relayed to the Oil & Gas
Division, Bismarck, via telephone. The following information must be included: Well name, legal location, permit number,
drilling contractor, company representative, date and time of spudding. Office hours are 8:00 a.m. to 12:00 p.m. and 1:00
p.m. to 5:00 p.m. Central Time. Our telephone number is (701) 328-8020, leave a message if after hours or on the weekend.
Survey Requirements for Horizontal, Horizontal Re-entry, and Directional Wells
NDAC Section 43-02-03-25 (Deviation Tests and Directional Surveys) states in part (that) the survey contractor shall file a
certified copy of all surveys with the director free of charge within thirty days of completion. Surveys must be submitted as
one electronic copy, or in a form approved by the director. However, the director may require the directional survey to be
filed immediately after completion if the survey is needed to conduct the operation of the director's office in a timely manner.
Certified surveys must be submitted via email in one adobe document, with a certification cover page to certsurvey@nd.gov.
Survey points shall be of such frequency to accurately determine the entire location of the well bore.
Specifically, the Horizontal and Directional well survey frequency is 100 feet in the vertical, 30 feet in the curve (or when
sliding) and 90 feet in the lateral.
Surface casing cement
Tail cement utilized on surface casing must have a minimum compressive strength of 500 psi within 12 hours, and tail
cement utilized on production casing must have a minimum compressive strength of 500 psi before drilling the plug or
initiating tests.
Logs NDAC Section 43-02-03-31 requires the running of (1) a suite of open hole logs from which formation tops and
porosity zones can be determined, (2) a Gamma Ray Log run from total depth to ground level elevation of the well bore, and
(3) a log from which the presence and quality of cement can be determined (Standard CBL or Ultrasonic cement evaluation
log) in every well in which production or intermediate casing has been set, this log must be run prior to completing the well.
All logs run must be submitted free of charge, as one digital TIFF (tagged image file format) copy and one digital LAS (log
ASCII) formatted copy. Digital logs may be submitted on a standard CD, DVD, or attached to an email sent to
digitallogs@nd.gov
Thank you for your cooperation.
Sincerely,
David Burns
Engineering Technician

Surveys are required at least every 30 feet in the build section and every 90 feet in the lateral section of a horizontal well. Measurement
inaccuracies are not considered when determining compliance with the spacing/drilling unit boundary setback requirement except in the

Oil and Gas Wells
Dear Operator,
North Dakota Administrative Code Section 43-02-03-19.2 states in part that all waste material associated with
exploration or production of oil and gas must be properly disposed of in an authorized facility in accord with all
applicable local, state, and federal laws and regulations.
Filtration systems are commonly used during oil and gas operations in North Dakota. The Commission is very
concerned about the proper disposal of used filters (including filter socks) used by the oil and gas industry.
Effective June 1, 2014, a container must be maintained on each well drilled in North Dakota beginning when the
well is spud and must remain on-site during clean-out, completion, and flow-back whenever filtration operations
are conducted. The on-site container must be used to store filters until they can be properly disposed of in an
authorized facility. Such containers must be:
leakproof to prevent any fluids from escaping the container
covered to prevent precipitation from entering the container
placard to indicate only filters are to be placed in the container
If the operator will not utilize a filtration system, a waiver to the container requirement will be considered, but
only upon the operator submitting a Sundry Notice (Form 4) justifying their request.
As previously stated in our March 13, 2014 letter, North Dakota Administrative Code Section 33-20-02.1-01
states in part that every person who transports solid waste (which includes oil and gas exploration and production
wastes) is required to have a valid permit issued by the North Dakota Department of Health, Division of Waste
Management. Please contact the Division of Waste Management at (701) 328-5166 with any questions on the
solid waste program. Note oil and gas exploration and production wastes include produced water, drilling mud,
invert mud, tank bottom sediment, pipe scale, filters, and fly ash.
Thank you for your cooperation.
Sincerely,
Bruce E. Hicks
Assistant Director
KODIAK OIL & GAS (USA), INC.

THE PROPOSED CASING AND CEMENTING PROGRAM
Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA), Inc. (Kodiak) proposes to drill a vertical hole in the NWNW (860'
FNL, 1285' FWL) of Section 16 in Township 155 North, Range 99 West to an approximate
TVD of 10445' (KOP) at which time directional drilling operations will be initiated on an
azimuth direction of 235° with a 12°/100 build and will end the build at 10936' TVD, 11290'
MD and an inclination of 90o located 1264' FNL and 903' FWL of Section 16 in Township
Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA) Inc.
P Dam State 155-99-4-16-21-13H3
155 North, Range 99 West, at which point the 7" intermediate casing will land. The
horizontal lateral azimuth will be held at 181° thru the Three Forks Pay with a hold of 90°
inclination. The projected bottom hole target location of 248' FSL and 540' FWL of Section
21Township 155 North, Range 99 West. Total length of lateral is approximately 9067'.

EVALUATION PROGRAM
Logs
: The logging program will be run by Schlumberger and will consist of their
Platform Express plus BHC Sonic from 11290' to 2149'. The Gamma Ray
and resistivity log will be pulled back to surface.
The evaluation program may change at the discretion of the well site geologist.
COMPLETION
The planned top of the 4.5" 11.6" production liner will be 50-100 ft above the kick off point.
This liner will be cemented throughout the lateral and a wet shoe sub will be set at the toe of the
liner. The frac string will be 12.75# P-110 with TSHP threads and stung into a PBR run with the
liner hanger prior to the fracturing treatment. Current plans are for 28 stages using a plug and
perf approach.
ABNORMAL CONDITIONS
No abnormal temperatures or pressures are anticipated. Hydrogen sulfide (H2S) may be
encountered in the Mission Canyon Formation at a TVD of approximately 9490'. Safety
equipment will be in operation at approximately 8990'.
Maximum anticipated bottom hole pressure equals approximately 5905psi (calculated at 0.54
psi/foot at Three Forks target depth, 10,936') and maximum anticipated surface pressure equals
approximately 3500psi (bottom hole pressure minus the pressure of a partially evacuated hole
calculated at 0.22 psi/foot at Three Forks target depth). The intermediate design casing will be
run and cemented before drilling the target lateral.
Reservoir temperature is anticipated to be 244F.

This notice is being provided to confirm that Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA), Inc. will be using no
diesel fuels, as defined by the United States Environmental Protection Agency (USEPA), in
the hydraulic fracturing of the above referenced wells.
USEPA identifies diesel fuel as any compound
identified with the following Chemical Abstract Service Registry Numbers:
68334-30-5 (Primary Name: Fuels, diesel)
68476-34-6 (Primary Name: Fuels, diesel, No. 2)
68476-30-2 (Primary Name: Fuel oil No. 2)
68476-31-3 (Primary Name: Fuel oil, No. 4)
8008-20-6 (Primary Name: Kerosene)
Should you have any questions or require additional information, please feel free to contact me.

APPROVAL
This Spill Prevention Control and Countermeasures (SPCC) Plan represents Kodiak Oil & Gas
(USA) Inc. (Kodiak's) plan for compliance with SPCC regulations found in 40 CFR, Part 112 for
the Onshore Production Facilities detailed in this plan. These Onshore Production Facilities (as
defined in § 112.2) are applicable not only to the general SPCC requirements in § 112.7, but
additionally are subject to § 112.9 that is specific to Onshore Production Facilities. This SPCC
Plan is a carefully thought-out document, prepared in accordance with good engineering
practices. A manager with the authority to commit resources necessary to implement the plan
has approved and signed this SPCC Plan below.
Management Approval (§112.7):
This Spill Prevention Control and Countermeasure Plan will be implemented as herein described.
Corrections or deficiencies found at Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA) Inc. (Kodiak) facilities that are
included in this plan, will be corrected to meet the minimal containment dimensions and repairs.

Spill Prevention Control and Countermeasure Plan
Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA) Inc.
May 2014
1.0
INTRODUCTION
The environmental and economic effects of oil spills can be best avoided through prevention and
containment. For more than two decades, the Environmental Protection Agency's (EPA) Spill
Prevention, Control, and Countermeasures (SPCC) program has worked at several hundred
thousand oil storage facilities to prevent the discharge of petroleum and non-petroleum oil into
Waters of the United States. The EPA's approach to preventing oil spills combines planning and
enforcement measures. To prevent oil spills, the EPA requires owners or operators of certain oil
storage facilities to prepare and implement SPCC Plans that detail the facility's spill prevention
and control measures.
Regulations addressed under 40 CFR Part 112 - Oil Pollution Prevention establish procedures,
methods, equipment and other requirements to prevent the discharge of oil from non-
transportation-related onshore and offshore facilities into or upon the navigable Waters of the
United States or adjoining shorelines.
This SPCC Plan represents Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA) Inc. (Kodiak's) standard plan for
compliance with SPCC regulations found in 40 CFR Part 112 for Onshore Production Facilities.
It is to be reviewed at least every 5 years and updated and re-certified, if necessary, or when
material changes occur. A record of these reviews is included in Appendix A. Any facility
owned or operated by Kodiak that does not meet an exception covered under 40 CFR Part 112, §
112.1 listed below must have an SPCC Plan.
1.
The equipment or operation of vessels or the facility is subject to authority and
control of the U.S. Department of Transportation.
2.
The facility has an aggregate storage of 1,320 gallons (31.4 bbls) or less of oil.
"Storage" is defined as capacity of storage vessels and not actual stored volume.
"Oil" is defined as oil in any form, including, but not limited to, petroleum, fuel
oil, sludge, oil refuse, and oil mixed with wastes other than dredged spoil.
Produced water is also considered to be an oil under the regulation. Only
containers with a capacity of 55 gallons or greater are included in this calculation.
3.
The facility has a total underground storage capacity of 42,000 gallons (1,000
bbls) or less.
4.
The facility, due to its location, could not reasonably be expected to discharge
into or upon navigable waters of the U.S. or adjoining shorelines, or waters of the
contiguous zone, or in connection with activities under the Outer Continental
Shelf Lands Act or Deepwater Port Act, or affecting certain natural resources.
"Navigable waters" are defined in Public Law 92-500 as interstate waters;
intrastate lakes, rivers, and streams (year-round or seasonal) which are utilized by
interstate travelers for recreational or other purposes; and intrastate lakes, rivers,
and streams (year-round or seasonal) from which fish or shellfish are taken and
sold in interstate commerce.
1
Spill Prevention Control and Countermeasure Plan
Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA) Inc.
May 2014
1.1
Facility Description (§112.7(a)(3))
The onshore production well pads detailed in the attachments to this plan are typically manned
for less than 24 hours and are used for fluid separation and storage of liquids from single or
multiple oil/gas/condensate wells. These well pads operate continuously, 365 days per year, with
periodic monitoring by trained operations personnel on a regular basis. A diagram of the
physical layout of each well pad is included as a plot plan in the site specific attachments at the
end of this Plan.
At a well pad, the raw production flows from the well head to a pressure vessel that separates the
flow into both liquid and gas phases. From the pressure vessel, the gas that is produced may
flow through various gas handling and/or mechanical equipment prior to delivery into a
gathering or sales pipeline. The liquids produced are then further separated into produced water
and oil (Note: For simplicity in this Plan the term `oil' is used to denote produced hydrocarbon
fluids, meaning either oil or gas condensate, produced water, and flowback fluids.) The oil and
produced water are stored in single or multiple tanks. Once a sufficient volume of oil has
accumulated, it is transferred into a tank truck and transported offsite or may be sent from the
location via pipeline. Periodically, the produced water is loaded into a tank truck and sent to a
produced water facility. The total volume of oil storage capacity at this facility is presented in
Table A-1 of Appendix A.
1.2
Surrounding Land Use
Surrounding property consists of the Epping-Springbrook Dam recreational area, rural residential
areas, and agricultural land. Flora consists of various native grasses and scrub vegetation.
1.3
Drainage Pathway and Distance to Navigable Waters
Drainage pathways and surface water bodies may be impacted by a release from these sites. A
current plot plan and a topographic map for each well pad are included in this Plan as site-
specific attachments. These figures provide information including the direction of flow, well pad
location, proximity to nearby drainage pathways, and surface water bodies. The direction of
flow listed on plot plans indicates the direction that a discharge would flow immediately from
the well pad. The direction shown on topographic maps indicates where the discharge would
ultimately flow as well as nearby water bodies.

Spill Prevention Control and Countermeasure Plan

2.0
POTENTIAL FAILURE ASSESSMENT (§112.7(b))
The reasonably expected modes of failure or accidents in which oil or produced water could be
discharged from various equipment or operations at these production facilities are presented
below. These sources of potential discharge are also addressed in Table 1 - Potential Failure
Assessment, which is included with the site-specific attachments for each well pad. The
following three issues are addressed for each potential source of failure.
i. Rate of flow: The estimated discharge rate of fluids that may occur in the event of a leak or
an overflow from a piece of equipment. Discharge rates for leaks or equipment failures are often
described as variable because many factors could contribute to the severity of a discharge.
Production rates, storage capacity, location of leak, and type of failure are all variables that could
affect the magnitude of a discharge.
ii. The total quantity of oil that may be discharged: The maximum release volume that may
be expected in the event of a leak or overflow from a piece of equipment. Detailed below is a
discussion of the volume of oil that could potentially be discharged from the different equipment
or operations that are commonly used at production facilities. In some instances, the release
volumes are identified as variable and are proportional to the time it takes to identify a discharge
or the fluid production rate.
iii. Direction of Flow: The initial direction of flow in the event of a discharge is indicated on
the well pad plot plan for each site-specific attachment. The ultimate direction of flow for a
discharge once it has migrated off-site is indicated on each site-specific topographic map.
A. Storage Tank Leak and Failure
Failures may result from corrosion, non-secure joints, settling combined with deformation of the
tank, or complete failure due to a fracture. Storage tanks include oil tanks, produced water tanks,
and other onsite portable or temporary tanks larger than 55 gallons.
i.
Rate of flow: Variable. Dependent on the size and location of tank failure.
ii.
Total quantity of oil that could be discharged: The total quantity of oil that could be
discharged would not exceed the working capacity of the largest storage tank (See Table
1 of each attachment).
iii.
Direction of flow: A spill from a tank leak or failure initially will be contained inside the
lined secondary containment structure that surrounds the tank.
(See secondary
containment dimensions on plot plan of each attachment.) Should a release escape from
the secondary containment structure, the liquid would pool in the immediate vicinity.
Larger volumes would ultimately be contained by a facility-wide berm system to prevent
the spill from migrating off location. Direction of flow is indicated on the plot plan and
the topographic map of each attachment.
3
Spill Prevention Control and Countermeasure Plan
Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA) Inc.
May 2014
B. Tank Overflow
Oil and produced water are removed from each production well pad on a regular basis. In the
event that the fluids were not removed in a timely manner, production would accumulate and
could potentially overfill the capacity of the storage tank(s).
i. Rate of flow: Rate is presented in Table 1 of each attachment, in barrels of oil per day
(BOPD) or barrels of water per day (BWPD). This estimate is based on assuming that
80% of the volume from a single storage tank is the maximum volume released for a tank
overflow incident.
ii. Total quantity of oil that could be discharged: Variable. The total quantity spilled is in
proportion to the length of time the tank is overflowing.
iii. Direction of flow: A spill from a tank overflow will be contained initially inside the
secondary containment structure that surrounds the tank. (See secondary containment
dimensions on plot plan of each attachment.) Should a release escape from the secondary
containment structure, the liquid would pool in the immediate vicinity. Larger volumes
would ultimately be contained by a facility-wide berm system to prevent the spill from
migrating off location. Direction of flow is indicated on the plot plan and the topographic
map of each attachment.
C. Oil/Produced Water Transfer Area
Oil and produced water may be transported by truck. In the tank truck loading area, there is
potential for the truck compartment to overflow during oil transfer. Another failure mode for
both oil and produced water transfer could occur when loading system piping is parted or broken
off during transfer from the tank to the truck compartment.
i. Rate of flow: The flow rate would be approximately 480 barrels of oil per hour (BOPH)
in the event of a truck compartment overflow based on the typical loading or pumping
rate of the truck. The maximum rate of flow would be approximately 480 BOPH if a
loading system component is accidently disconnected while loading.
ii. Total quantity of oil that could be discharged: The total quantity of oil that could be
discharged is the largest compartment on a tank truck, which is not expected to exceed
180 barrels for any oil hauling truck utilized in the operation. (180 bbl compartments are
anticipated by the trucks visiting these facilities.)
Load out operations are manned;
therefore, personnel would close valves on both the tank truck and the bulk storage
container thereby controlling and minimizing the quantity of oil discharged.
iii. Direction of flow: The tank loading area is flat, thus oil spilled from a truck related
failure would be released in and around the loading area. Load out operations are always
observed by trucking personnel, and thus a loading release would be noted immediately.
Should a release occur, however, the liquid would pool in the immediate vicinity. Larger
volumes would ultimately be contained by a facility-wide berm system to prevent the
spill from migrating off location. Direction of flow is indicated on the plot plan and the
topographic map of each attachment. Due to loading operations being under constant
supervision, it is unlikely that a large volume spill would occur.
4
Spill Prevention Control and Countermeasure Plan
Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA) Inc.
May 2014
D. Pressure Vessel Failure
Failures or leaks from pressure vessels may occur due to corrosion, cracked seams, pin hole
leaks, pop-offs or other equipment failures. Due to operating conditions under pressure, failures
or leaks from pressure vessels may not be contained within the provided general secondary
containment depending on their location and the direction of the discharge.
i.
Rate of flow: Variable. Dependent on the size of the pressure vessel and mode of
failure. The total maximum rate of flow expected from a pressure vessel failure is not
anticipated to exceed the amount of total liquid per day presented in Table 1 of each
attachment plus fluid volume of the vessel above the elevation of rupture.
ii.
Total quantity of liquid that could be discharged: Variable. Depends on the rate of flow
entering the pressure vessel and the location of the failure. The maximum potential
release would not exceed the quantity noted in Table 1.
iii.
Direction of flow: A spill from a pressure vessel leak or failure would be initially
contained inside the general secondary containment structure that surrounds the vessel.
Should a release escape from the containment structure or extend beyond the confines of
the secondary containment, the liquid would pool in the immediate vicinity. Larger
volumes would ultimately be contained by a facility-wide berm system to prevent the
spill from migrating off location. Direction of flow is indicated on the plot plan and the
topographic map of each attachment.
E. Piping Failure
Piping failures may result from general corrosion, including pitting and flaking, deterioration of
seals on valves, and piping connection failures. The majority of well pad piping is confined
either inside secondary containment with the bulk storage containers/pressure vessels or is below
ground between the wellhead, the processing equipment, and the tanks/vessels.
Above and below ground piping are also associated with LACT units at applicable sites. LACT
skids contain a floor drain/drip pan system to contain small spills. The majority of LACT skids
are located within secondary containment. Oil is not stored by LACT units, which consist of
piping, pumps, and meters to measure oil produced. LACT unit operation is automated. Failures
may result from pump or seal malfunction or deterioration.
i. Rate of flow: Variable. Dependent on size and location of a piping related failure.
Maximum expected potential rate of flow is not anticipated to exceed the amount of total
liquid per day presented in Table 1 of each attachment. Personnel routinely perform
visual inspection of intra-facility piping and buried flowline right-of-ways to detect any
failures.
ii. Total quantity of oil that could be discharged: Variable. Depends on the rate of flow and
the location of the failure. The maximum potential release would not exceed the quantity
noted in Table 1 of each respective well pad (see site specific attachments).
iii. Direction of flow: See plot plan and topographic map of each attachment.
5
Spill Prevention Control and Countermeasure Plan
Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA) Inc.

2.1
Appropriate Containment and/or Diversion Structures (§112.7(c))
Table 2 of each site specific well pad attachment identifies bulk storage containers, production
equipment, and a significant portion of the aboveground piping that are protected by appropriate
secondary containment to prevent releases from entering or potentially entering navigable waters
and/or Waters of the U.S. In addition, the plot plans in the site specific well pad attachments
illustrate the shape and location of the secondary containment.
Where sized secondary containment is required, berm capacity calculations are provided in the
site specific attachments to show that the constructed secondary containment has adequate
storage capacity to hold the volume of the largest container plus sufficient freeboard. For SPCC
compliance, freeboard is based on a precipitation allowance from a 25-year, 24-hour storm event.
For well pads located in North Dakota, State requirements for freeboard are calculated based on
a volume allowance for one day's fluid production (NDIC 43-02-03-49).
2.2
Conformance with SPCC Requirements (§112.7(a)(1))
Whenever possible, steps have been taken to provide all vessels that contain oil with secondary
containment to prevent oil from entering the Waters of the U.S. In order to contain a potential
spill from bulk storage containers, pressure vessels, and the majority of the above ground piping,
secondary containment structures have been constructed, when feasible, and are maintained to
prevent discharges from reaching the Waters of the U.S. More detailed information regarding
the size, capacity and locations of these structures can be found in the site specific attachments.
Whenever secondary containment has been determined impracticable as per §112.7(d) or
instances where alternative measures are employed in lieu of secondary containment, as per
§112.9(c), an Oil Spill Contingency Plan (emergency response plan) has been prepared as well as
a commitment of manpower, equipment, and materials to contain and cleanup the discharge.
Deviations from SPCC requirements are outlined in Section 2.3.
In addition to the installation of secondary containment structures, the following steps have been
taken to prevent the discharge of oil to the environment:
Assessments of potential failures for bulk storage containers, transfer areas, pressure
vessels, and piping have been made. (See well pad specific attachments, Table 1)
Procedures for well pad inspections and personnel training are in-place. (See Appendix
C and Section 4.0)
Kodiak has committed to providing a strong spill prevention program. (See Appendix B)
A list of emergency contacts and contractors is provided. (See Appendix F and Appendix
G)
Spill kits are provided at many well pad locations
6
Spill Prevention Control and Countermeasure Plan
Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA) Inc.

2.3
Deviations from SPCC Requirements (§112.7(a)(2))
2.3.1 Provisions Provided in lieu of Secondary Containment (§112.7(d))
Where secondary containment has been deemed impracticable, alternative measures have been
taken to provide environmental protection from oil and produced water discharges. This section
outlines situations and equipment where the installation of secondary containment is
impracticable and the basis for this determination. Detailed below are the alternative measures
that are implemented in lieu of secondary containment:
An Oil Spill Contingency Plan following the provisions of Part 109 of 40 CFR
(Appendix I)
A written commitment of manpower, equipment, and materials required to
expeditiously control and remove any quantity of oil discharge that may be harmful
(Appendix B).
A flowline Maintenance Program detailing visual inspections and/or tests for leaks;
record keeping measures; assurance that the equipment is compatible with the
production fluids (corrosiveness, volume, and operational pressure); corrective action
and repairs associated with a discharge; and prompt removal of oil accumulated
during a discharge (Appendix H).
Spill reporting procedures (Appendix F) and emergency contractor list (Appendix G)
Procedures for well pad inspections and personnel training (See Appendix C and
Section 4.0)
Spill kits are provided at many well pad locations
2.3.1.1 Loadout/Transfer Areas
Loadout/Transfer Areas: Constructed secondary containment for the loading/unloading areas is
impracticable the following reasons:
Secondary containment, including berms and drainage systems are impracticable because
the Loadout/Transfer Area must be easily accessed by the tanker truck. Placement of
berms and or drainage systems around the Loadout/Transfer Areas would act as a
physical barrier to access and could potentially become a safety issue.
Berms established in the Loadout/Transfer Area could confine rain water, thereby
impeding the Operator's ability to access the site.
Loadout/Transfer areas are located on level earthen ground, and only a minimal volume
of oil or produced water would be discharged during a transfer malfunction. Thus, the
probability of a discharge to navigable waters as described in §112.1(b)(1) should be
minimal.
7
Spill Prevention Control and Countermeasure Plan
Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA) Inc.
May 2014
2.3.1.2 Mechanical Rotating Equipment (§112.7(k))
Mechanical Rotating Equipment:
Mechanical rotating equipment (oil-filled operational
equipment) includes, but is not limited to, engine drivers, compressor frames, and pump jack
crank cases. Secondary containment is impracticable for mechanical rotating equipment for the
following reasons:
Establishing passive secondary containment structures around mechanical rotating
equipment would limit access to the equipment, therefore making maintenance
operations unnecessarily difficult.
Active secondary containment may not be feasible because the well pad is generally
un-manned.
2.3.1.3 Flowlines and Intra-facility Gathering Lines (§112.9(d)(3))
Flowlines and intra-facility gathering lines may be above and below ground. Piping associated
with bulk storage tanks, pressure vessels, and LACT units is typically located inside secondary
containment structures. In most cases it is impracticable to provide secondary containment for
flowlines and intra-facility gathering lines due to:
The potential impacts to the surrounding areas (public and agricultural lands).
Secondary containment provided for on-site piping may impede operational activities
and create a safety hazard for operational personnel.
Active secondary containment may not be feasible because the well pad is generally
unmanned.
8
Spill Prevention Control and Countermeasure Plan
Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA) Inc.
May 2014
3.0
INSPECTION AND RECORDS (§112.7(e))
Examinations: Visual observations by field personnel and other qualified personnel.
Inspections: Annual SPCC compliance inspection and/or appraisal of remaining
equipment life by qualified/certified personnel.
3.1
Routine Operational Examinations
Routine operational examinations of tanks, separators, heater treaters, piping, supports, and
foundations will be conducted at each facility, as required. These examinations are typically
performed by field operators during their routine on-site visits and are un-documented, unless an
abnormality is observed.
If problems are identified, appropriate corrective actions will be taken to remedy the issue.
Corrective action needs to be taken as soon as practicable but no later than six months from the
identification of the issue.
Guidance to perform routine operational examinations as well as specific guidance for the
internal/external examination of storage tanks and process vessels and the external examination
of process piping is presented in Appendix C. All Guidance has been developed to comply with
applicable industry standards, as required by §112.3(d).
3.2
Annual SPCC Inspections
A comprehensive SPCC inspection will be performed annually at each facility. These
inspections must be performed by a qualified inspector and will be documented according to the
annual SPCC checklist found in Appendix C of this plan.
Inspection procedures are re-evaluated every 5 years during the SPCC plan review. A
description of the review process is detailed below in Section 3.4.1.
Records of annual inspections are maintained at the field and/or corporate office. If problems are
identified, appropriate corrective actions will be noted on the inspection form, and prompt action
will be taken for repairs or replacement.
Outlined below are the typical procedures for conducting an annual SPCC inspection:
Use inspection forms provided in Appendix C. Document the date of the inspection.
Conduct a walking inspection of the well pad, including but not limited to visual external
inspections of the following in accordance to the guidelines on the form in Appendix C:
o Storage Tanks
o Secondary Containment Structures
o Production Equipment
o Process Piping
Inspection forms are maintained at the field and/or corporate office.

3.4
Plan Reviews
3.4.1 Plan Reviews Due to Material Changes (§112.5(a))
In addition to the scheduled reviews of the SPCC plan, the plan will be reviewed whenever a
material change is made to the well pad (any change that may affect the potential for a
discharge). If a material change is made to the well pad, the plan will be amended to reflect the
changes, and the material change will be recertified by a professional engineer. A record of
these reviews can be found in Appendix A (`SPCC Plan Review Records ­ Site Modifications")
and the recertification by a professional engineer can be found at the beginning of the plan (page
v).
3.4.2 5 Year Plan Review (§112.5(b))
At least once every five years, the SPCC Plan will be reviewed and inspection procedures will be
re-evaluated to verify compliance based upon changes in regulations, industry standards,
personnel and administration, and appropriate technology. If issues are identified, appropriate
corrective actions will be determined and the SPCC Plan and/or inspection procedures will be
modified.

for three years.

PERSONNEL TRAINING AND SPILL PREVENTION PROCEDURES
4.1
Training of Oil Handling Personnel (§112.7(f)(1))
Facility personnel that will typically be involved in oil-handling operations are properly trained
in the operation and maintenance of equipment and operating procedures to prevent oil
discharges. In addition, oil-handling personnel are trained in the pollution control laws, rules
and regulations and the contents of this SPCC plan.
4.2
Designated Individual Responsible for Implementation of SPCC Plan (§112.7(f)(2))
The individual responsible for implementing this SPCC Plan, discharge prevention, and
reporting to facility management is indicated in the General Information section of this Plan
(page i).
4.3
Discharge Prevention Training (§112.7(f)(3))
Company and contract personnel attend an in-house discharge prevention training session on an
annual basis to assure adequate understanding of the SPCC plan and associated issues for the
production facilities.
Spill related topics discussed at training may include:

Known spill events;

Malfunctioning equipment;

Spill control equipment;

Operation and maintenance of equipment to prevent discharges;

Inspection of containment structures, vessels, tanks and piping;

Spill response containment and clean-up;

Company policies on reporting and responding to spills and on SPCC Plans;

Applicable pollution control laws, rules and regulations; and

Updates on State and Federal regulations, company policies and procedures, and
spill reporting
A review of known spill events or failures, malfunctioning components, and recently developed
precautionary measures may also be included in the training. Documentation of training session
attendance is maintained at the field and/or corporate office.
Additional short briefing sessions may be held, as needed, before and during certain jobs to
review spill potential, necessary precautions, and probable responses.

5.0
FACILITY DRAINAGE (§112.9(b))
All secondary containment structures are inspected on a regular basis for damage and for
accumulations of oil, produced water, and precipitation. No written record is maintained for
these routine operational examinations.
Generally, there are no installed provisions for drainage from the secondary containment
structures for storage tanks and process vessels. Devices penetrating containment structures
intended for drainage are not installed due to possibility for an accidental discharge of fluids
from within the containment structure. Minor accumulations of precipitation are allowed to
evaporate. Large accumulations of precipitation and/or produced water may be removed with a
vacuum truck and returned to the separation vessels for treatment or removed to a permitted
disposal facility.
If a well pad is located within proximity to surface water, an earthen berm may be provided
around the well pad to provide additional protection to Waters of the U.S. Any drainage valve
connecting discharge from an on-site detention area or drainage swale to the exterior of a well
pad berm will typically remain closed.
Drainage ditches in and around the facilities and roadside ditches within the field are observed
by field operators during routine operational examinations. If pollution or evidence of a spill is
detected, the source will be identified and stopped. The ditch will be isolated by constructing an
earthen dam or other suitable containment, and the oil or other pollutant will be removed or
treated, as appropriate. The applicable agency will be informed as required by regulation based
on the type of fluid and volumes released. Procedures for reporting a spill are addressed in
Section 12.3 and the spill reporting flowchart in Appendix F.

Spill Prevention Control and Countermeasure Plan
Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA) Inc.
May 2014
6.0
BULK STORAGE TANKS (§112.9(c))
At these facilities, bulk storage tanks are utilized to store oil and produced water. (Produced
water and flowback fluids are regulated as an oil under the regulation.) In addition, portable or
temporary oil storage tanks are also regulated as bulk storage tanks. The number and size of
fixed tanks at each site are presented in Table 1 of the well pad attachments. Their approximate
location and relationship to other on-site equipment is illustrated in the plot plan provided for
each site specific attachment.
6.1
Tank Design (§112.9(c)(1))
Oil storage tanks are designed to be compatible with the material and conditions of storage. Oil
storage tanks are vertical fixed roof tanks, cylindrical in shape, constructed of steel to API 12F
specifications or equivalent. Produced water tanks are vertical fixed roof tanks, cylindrical in
shape, and constructed of fiberglass to API 12P specifications or equivalent. The tanks are
painted to prevent corrosion. The total volume of the tanks is sufficient for production inflow
rates considering the time interval between routine visits of operational personnel.
6.2
Secondary Containment Design (§112.9(c)(2))
Sized secondary containment is required for all bulk storage containers, including all portable or
temporary oil storage containers larger than 55 gallons. For tank batteries, earthen material or
steel structures may be used to form barriers around all bulk storage containers. Earthen berms
are constructed of locally available soil with side slopes of approximately 45o. Steel constructed
structures are fabricated to contain oil; any joint or protrusions through the berm wall are also
designed to retain fluids. Portable or temporary storage tanks typically utilize a variety of pre-
fabricated, readily available containers for secondary containment.
The volume of the secondary containment berm is sized to contain the volume of the largest tank
plus a sufficient allowance for freeboard. For SPCC compliance, freeboard is based on a
precipitation allowance from a 25-year, 24-hour storm event. In addition, the State of North
Dakota has a requirement for secondary containment to provide sufficient volume to contain the
volume of the largest tank plus one day's fluid production (NDIC 43-02-03-49). For calculation
purposes, one day's fluid production is used as the average daily production from the single
highest producing well associated with the well pad. Berm volumes are designed to contain the
larger of the above freeboard criteria, as applicable.
6.3
Examinations, Inspections, and Recordkeeping (§112.9(c)(3))
Production operators perform routine operational examinations of each tank for indication of
leaks, abnormalities, containment deterioration, or equipment malfunctions during their daily
visit to the well pad. Should daily visits not be performed, routine operational examinations will
be performed no less than monthly.
Examinations and inspections may be performed as directed in API RP 12R1. Any necessary
maintenance or repairs should also follow the industry standard API RP 12R1, Recommended
14
Spill Prevention Control and Countermeasure Plan
Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA) Inc.
May 2014
Practice for Setting, Maintenance, Inspection, Operation, and Repair of Tanks in Production
Service. Examination and inspection guidance based on this standard are included in Appendix
C.
6.4
Tank Battery Design (§112.9(c)(4))
Tank battery installations are engineered in accordance with good engineering practices to
prevent failures and discharges. Tanks are equipped with equalizer lines of adequate size for
normal inflow rates and for overflow protection, where multiple tanks are present. Overpressure
protection is also utilized in the form of a tank relief/vent valve and/or an API vacuum/vent
gauge hatch. The API vacuum/vent gauge hatch also provides vacuum protection during load
out.

Spill Prevention Control and Countermeasure Plan
Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA) Inc.

7.0
PRESSURE/PROCESS VESSELS (§112.9(c))
At these facilities, flow-through pressure and/or process vessels are utilized for the
separation of produced fluids. Oil and produced water flow into, through and out of these
vessels by interconnecting piping. The primary purpose of these flow-through process
vessels is to separate well fluids into its marketable phases and include, but are not
limited to heater treaters, separators, and free-water knockouts.
7.1
Pressure/Process Vessel Design and Maintenance (§112.9(c)(1))
Pressure vessels are designed to meet ASME Boiler and Pressure Vessel Code, Section
VIII. Process vessels are typically designed to either the ASME Section VIII code and/or
the applicable API specification (i.e., 12J, 12K, 12GDU, 12L).
Maintenance of pressure or process vessels should at a minimum follow Section 8.0 of
API RP 510, Alternative Rules for Exploration and Production Pressure Vessels.
7.2
Secondary Containment Design (§112.9(c)(2) and (c)(5))
General secondary containment is typically provided for all pressure/process vessels.
Earthen material or steel structures may be used to form barriers around pressure/process
vessels. Earthen berms are constructed of locally available soil with side slopes of
approximately 45o and are designed to retain fluids. Steel constructed structures are
fabricated to contain oil, and any joint or protrusions through the berm wall are also
designed to retain fluids.
Secondary containment for pressure/process vessels may adhere to either sized or general
containment design requirements. Kodiak provides general secondary containment for
flow through process vessels in the form of earthen or steel berms. (Berm calculations
supporting sized containment are not provided for these vessels.) Kodiak adheres to the
below requirements to provide general secondary containment in lieu of sized
containment:
Provide general secondary containment to address the most likely volume
of oil that would be discharged and/or produced by a typical failure event;
Take corrective action to repair issues identified during inspections;
Stablize/remove accumulated oil; and
Provide sized secondary containment should two spills larger than 42
gallons or one spill larger than 1,000 gallons occur within a 12 month
period.
7.3
Examinations, Inspections, and Recordkeeping (112.9(c)(3), 112.7(d))
Production operators perform routine operational examinations on the pressure/process
vessels for indication of leaks, abnormalities, or equipment malfunctions during their
16
Spill Prevention Control and Countermeasure Plan
Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA) Inc.
May 2014
routine visit to the well pad. Should routine visits not be performed, routine operational
examinations will be performed no less than monthly.
Additional inspections are warranted only when routine operational examinations provide
indication that a leak or malfunction has or could occur. Guidance on the items to
address in these examinations and inspections is included in Section 3.0 and Appendix C.
These guidance documents have been based on the industry best practices indentified in
Section 9.0 of the American Petroleum Institute's (API's) Inspection Code 510,
Alternative Rules for Exploration and Production Pressure Vessels.
Environmental equivalence in the form of integrity testing is not required for pressure

8.0
ANNCILARY ONSITE EQUIPMENT AND STRUCTURES
Oil, natural gas, and produced water flow from the well head, into and out of pressure
vessels, and the fluids then flow to bulk storage tanks through interconnecting piping.
Piping from the well head and pressure vessels may be either above or below ground.
From the pressure vessels, the natural gas can flow though a number of gas handling
devices before being sent down a sales line. In addition, mechanical rotating equipment
may exist at production facilities to aid in the production and distribution of fluids and
gases.
8.1
Facility Piping and LACT Units
8.1.1 Design and Maintenance
New piping is typically designed and installed to either ASME B31.3 or 31.4
specifications. Existing piping is expected to have been installed to similar standards.
Many well pads are being equipped with LACT units to measure oil production. LACT
installations consist of piping, pumps, and meters.
Maintenance of interconnecting piping at production facilities does not have a specific
applicable industry standard. Therefore, API 14E, Design and Installation of Offshore
Production Platform Piping System, is a reasonable standard to follow due to the
applicability of the specific industry and processes. Section 6.9 of this standard identifies
that API RP 510 contains guidelines on the topics of maintenance, inspection, and repair,
which can also be applied to piping systems.
8.1.2 Secondary Containment (§112.7(c), 112.9(d)(3))
In most cases, it is impracticable to provide secondary containment for flowlines and
intra-facility gathering lines due to probable impedance of on-site operational activities
and/or potential impacts to the surrounding areas (public and agricultural lands). In lieu
of secondary containment, the alternative measures described in Section 2.3.1 have been
provided.
LACT units are provided with general secondary containment in the form of berms. In
addition, the LACT unit is constructed with an internal containment system comprised of
floor drains discharging to a containment skid to minimize the risk of discharge. The
containment skid has a discharge valve that remains closed. Contained oil is pumped out
for removal.
8.1.3 Examinations, Inspections, and Recordkeeping
Operators perform routine operational examinations of above ground interconnecting
piping for indication of leaks, abnormalities, or equipment malfunctions during their
routine well pad visit. Should routine visits not be performed, routine operational
examinations will be performed no less than monthly.
18
Spill Prevention Control and Countermeasure Plan
Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA) Inc.
May 2014
Unscheduled examinations and inspections will be performed, as needed (refer to
Appendix C). Additional examinations and inspections are typically warranted only
when routine operational examinations indicate that a leak or malfunction has or could
occur. Guidance on the items to address in these examinations and inspections is
included in Appendix C.
These guidance documents have been based on the industry best practices indentified in
the American National Standards Institute (ANSI) and the American Petroleum
Institute's (API's) Inspection Code 574, Inspection Practices for Piping System
Components.
8.2
Mechanical Rotating Equipment (§112.7(k))
Mechanical rotating equipment, including engine drivers, compressor frames, and pump
jack crank cases, are common equipment for production facilities. These pieces of
equipment aid in the extraction, distribution and production of oil, produced water and
natural gas at onshore production facilities.
8.2.1 Secondary Containment (§112.7(c), 112.7(k))
Engine drivers, compressor frames, and pump jack crank cases contain oil solely for
lubrication, which is intrinsic to the operation of the equipment, and is considered to be
oil-filled operational equipment. In addition, equipment covered by this section is not
designed to store oil, and therefore is not considered to be a bulk storage container
subject to the mandatory secondary containment design requirements of §112.9(c). It has
been determined that general secondary containment is impracticable for this equipment.
In lieu of general secondary containment, the measures outlined in section 2.3.1 have
been taken to ensure potential discharges do not enter Waters of the U.S.
8.2.2 Examinations and Inspections
Production operators perform routine operational examinations on the mechanical
rotating equipment for indication of leaks, abnormalities, or equipment malfunctions
during their daily visit to the well pad. Should daily visits not be performed, routine
operational examinations will be performed no less than monthly.

Spill Prevention Control and Countermeasure Plan
Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA) Inc.

9.0
BRITTLE FRACTURE EVALUATION (§112.7(i))
Based on the material, construction, operational, and environmental parameters
associated with the equipment located at these central tank battery installations, there is a
very low risk of failure due to brittle fracture. The metal shell thicknesses of the heater
treaters, separators, free water knock-outs, welded tanks, and steel bolted tanks are not
thick enough to be susceptible to brittle fracture in either field repair or initial
manufacture.
It is recommended that a qualified welding inspector review any proposed field repairs
that would require welding or cutting of any of the vessels or bulk storage containers to
ensure that appropriate procedures are followed. Based on good engineering practice, the
requirements for repairs, alterations, reconstruction, or change in service should be in
accordance with the applicable API standard for repair of pressure vessels, steel welded
tanks, or steel bolted tanks (i.e., API RP 12R1, RP510).
9.1
Bulk Storage Containers
The steel storage tanks used at these facilities that may have potential for brittle fracture
failures are vertical fixed roof tanks constructed to API 12F specifications. The thickest
plate for an API 12 series tank is less than 0.5 inches, which is the necessary thickness in
which brittle fracture could be induced. This critical wall thickness is confirmed from
actual experience, as referenced in Appendix C of API RP 12R1. Thus, brittle fracture is
not a concern for API 12 series tanks unless they are operating in Arctic service. The
environmental conditions for these facilities would not be considered arctic in nature,
although cold temperatures are experienced in this region.
The steel bolted storage tanks used at these facilities do not have the potential for brittle
fracture to be induced. However, to ensure that brittle fracture is not an issue should a
field repair or alteration be required, a welding inspector should be consulted on the
repair method and qualifications associated with tanks in this service.
9.2
Pressure Vessels
Pressure vessels that may have potential for brittle fracture failures have been constructed
to ASME Boiler and Pressure Vessel Code Section VIII. Process vessels (i.e., glycol
dehydration units, heated separators) that may have potential for brittle fracture failures
are typically constructed to API 12K and 12GDU specifications. If such construction
standards have been followed, the appropriate precautions should have been taken to
reduce or eliminate the potential for brittle fracture failures. Field repairs should follow
the API recommended practice for the type of vessel service.
9.3
Piping and Fittings
Typical piping and fittings used at these facilities would not be susceptible to brittle
fracture failure due to the size, type of service, and environmental conditions they are
20
Spill Prevention Control and Countermeasure Plan
Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA) Inc.
May 2014
subjected to. Should piping of a greater thickness be required, appropriate stress reliving
techniques would be utilized to minimize any potential for brittle fracture failure.

Spill Prevention Control and Countermeasure Plan
Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA) Inc.

10.0
FACILITY TRANSFER AND PUMPING OPERATIONS (§112.9(d))
Truck loading/unloading operations are performed for oil and produced water at all
production facilities included in this Plan. Transfer procedures meet the minimum
requirements and regulation of the DOT. A copy of the Loading/Unloading Policy is
included in Appendix D of this Plan.
10.1
Examinations, Inspections and Maintenance (§112.9(d)(1))
All aboveground valves and pipelines related to production facilities receive routine
operational examinations by operating personnel at which time the general condition of
items, such as flange joints, valve bodies, pipeline supports, locking of valves, and metal
surfaces are assessed. When a pipeline is not in service for an extended period of time,
the connection at the transfer point is capped or blank-flanged.
Buried piping installations have a protective wrapping and coating. Sections of buried
line are carefully examined for deterioration in the event they are exposed. If corrosion
damage is found, additional examination and corrective action is taken, as indicated by
the magnitude of the damage.
Pipe supports are kept to a minimum by keeping pipe runs short and using pipe capable
of carrying the span without support. If supports are necessary, they allow for applicable
expansion and contraction while minimizing potential abrasive conditions.
Aboveground piping is guarded if it appears to be exposed to vehicular traffic hazards.
Typically, piping is located within bermed areas or installed close to structures to
minimize exposure to vehicular traffic.
10.2
Flowline Maintenance Program (§112.9(d)(3))
A Flowline Maintenance Program is in-place to minimize discharges from flow lines and
intra-facility gathering lines. A copy of the flowline maintenance program is included in
Appendix H.
This program is provided in addition to annual SPCC inspections and routine operational
examinations. The Flowline Maintenance Program is composed of multiple practices,
operations and policies that as a whole ensure the integrity and on-going maintenance of
well pad piping. The Flowline Maintenance Program is designed in accordance with the
industry standards listed in Section 8.1.
It has been assured that all valves, piping, and equipment are compatible with the type,
corrosivity, and operational volumes and pressures of fluids conveyed and reasonably
expected at these facilities. Visual inspections and/or tests of flowlines will occur on a
periodic and frequent schedule and if discharges are discovered, corrective action and
repairs will be promptly made and any accumulated oil will be stabilized and properly
remediated.
22
Spill Prevention Control and Countermeasure Plan
Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA) Inc.
May 2014
Inspection records, procedures, and maintenance records associated with the Flow Line
Maintenance Program are available at the field and/or corporate office.
10.3
Secondary Containment for Loadout/Transfer Areas (§112.7(c))
Loadout/Transfer areas are present at all Production Facilities and are intended to
facilitate the transport or disposal of fluids. Loadout/Transfer areas are designed for fluid
transport. However, these areas are not loading/unloading racks and are not subject to the
secondary containment requirements of §112.7(h). Loading/unloading racks typically
consist of permanent piping, assemblages, valves, loading arms, and pumping systems
necessary to load or unload tank trucks or cars. In contrast, production tank batteries
typically utilize a single hose and connection to facilitate the transfer of liquids from one
bulk storage container to a tank truck container. Loading/unloading racks are not present
at the production facilities listed in this Plan.
Although loading/unloading areas are not subject to the loading/unloading rack
requirements of § 112.7(h), general secondary containment is required and provided in
the form of drip pans at the loadout point to prevent minor spills. Drip pans are placed
directly beneath load line connections either within or just outside of the secondary
containment provided for the bulk storage tanks. The conditions of the drip pans are
observed by field operators during routine operational examinations. If a significant
quantity of oil, produced water, or rainwater has accumulated inside the drip pan, the
fluid will be removed with a vacuum truck for disposal.
10.4
Salt Water Disposal Facility (§112.9(d)(2)
Salt water disposal facilities will be examined on a regular basis, particularly following a
sudden change in atmospheric temperature to detect possible system upsets capable of
causing a discharge. All piping, injection pumps, and ancillary equipment will receive
routine operational examinations to assess the general condition of equipment, in
accordance with procedures outlined in Section 3.0.
23
Spill Prevention Control and Countermeasure Plan
Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA) Inc.
May 2014
11.0
DRILLING AND WORKOVER FACILITIES (§112.10)
Drilling and workover activities are subcontracted to third party well drilling and
servicing companies. These companies are responsible for complying with all spill
prevention regulations described in §112.10 that are applicable to their operations, if the
total well pad above ground storage of petroleum products is greater than 1,320 gallons
(31.4 bbls). At a minimum, the following provisions will be made:
Drilling and workover equipment are positioned or located so as to minimize the
potential of spilled oil, fuel, or oily drilling fluids from reaching navigable waters.
If necessary, the use of catchment basins or diversion structures will be
implemented.
A blowout preventor (BOP) will be installed before drilling and workover
operations, as required by well conditions and/or state regulations. The BOP
assembly will be capable of controlling the expected wellhead pressure.

12.0
SPILLS/ACCIDENT CONTINGENCY PLANNING (§112.7(d))
12.1
Spill History
This facility was not in service prior to January 10, 1974 (effective date of 40 CFR, Part
112A); however, a Spill History Form is included in Appendix E of this SPCC Plan for
purposes of spill recordkeeping at this facility. The corporate and/or field office
maintains spill reporting records.
12.2
Spill Response
Spill prevention procedures consist primarily of proper operation of equipment at each
well pad. All personnel have extensive experience operating the equipment, and the
tanks are monitored daily. In addition, an Oil Spill Contingency Plan has been developed
and is included in Appendix I of this Plan. Spill kits may be maintained at well pads in
the heater treater shed.
12.3
Spill Notification
Any spill that enters a watercourse or threatens to enter a watercourse no matter
the quantity is required to be reported to the EPA National Response Center at
(800) 424-8802.
In the event of a spill or release, the internal spill reporting protocol outlined in Appendix
F will be followed immediately. "The person responsible for discharge prevention" (see
pg. i) will determine whether the spill is of a reportable volume and which applicable
agencies to contact, if any. Appendix E provides a Discharge Notification Form that may
be used in the event of a discharge for internal reporting purposes and submitted to "the
person responsible for discharge prevention" (see pg. i).
The following information must typically be available when reporting a spill to a Federal
and/or State agency:
- Facility name;
- Facility owner/operator;
- Legal description of location where spill or release occurred;
- Contact phone number;
- Date and time of spill/release;
- Description material spilled or released and source of discharged;
- Estimated quantity discharged, as described in (§112.1(b));
- Cause of spill/release;
- Direction of flow of spill/release and distance traveled;
- Identification of any affected areas, including dry draws, and volume
involved;
- Actions taken to control and/or contain spill/release;
- Number and type of injuries, if any;
25
Spill Prevention Control and Countermeasure Plan
Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA) Inc.
May 2014
- Any damages that occurred as a result of the release;
- Weather conditions at the incident location;
- Names of individuals and/or organizations who have been contacted; and
- Other pertinent information specific to the spill/release.
In the event of a spill/discharge of any size, refer to the spill reporting flowchart that is
included in Appendix F for the required reporting procedures. Appendix F outlines the
State and Federal agencies and contact information to report spills/discharges of various
sizes.
12.4
Cleanup of Oil Spills & Conformance with State Requirements (§ 112.7(j))
Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA) Inc. (Kodiak) does not maintain the equipment or supplies
necessary to contain or cleanup large oil spills. A list of contractors and suppliers to be
contacted in the event of a spill is included in Appendix G of this SPCC Plan.
The responsible person (Section 4.2) must complete any approved corrective action by a
regulatory agency for releases that endanger public health or the environment. Operators
are also responsible for corrective action for non-reportable spills.
The North Dakota Industrial Commission (NDIC) Oil and Gas Division and the North
Dakota Department of Health (NDDOH) provide guidance on determining sensitive areas
and cleanup levels. This section is a brief overview of Kodiak's spill contingency plan;
however, a more detailed process is presented on the North Dakota Department of
Health's website, http://www.ndhealth.gov/WQ/GW/spills.htm.
12.4.1 Spills onto Soil
Oil spills should be contained as soon as possible by the construction of earthen dams or
by the placement of mechanical barriers. Free oil may be removed from the ground by
the use of a vacuum truck. Sumps or trenches may be dug to intercept or drain free oil.
Remaining free oil may be removed from the ground by the use of oil-absorbent
materials.
When all free oil has been removed, the affected soils containing over 100 milligrams per
kilogram (mg/kg or ppm) total petroleum hydrocarbon (TPH) should be delineated, both
vertically and horizontally. All soil containing over 100 ppm of TPH should then be
brought to the surface by excavating with a backhoe or other similar device for in-situ
remediation or disposal at a State approved landfill. If soil containing over 100 ppm TPH
is not disposed in a timely manner, it should be removed to a secure interim storage
location unless more immediate, on-site remediation techniques can be implemented.
A final cleanup level of 100 ppm should be achieved as soon as possible. Several
methods are acceptable for the cleanup of oil-contaminated soil, and regulatory agencies
may specify which method to use for the cleanup. NDDOH guidance on investigating
and remediating spills is found at:
26
Spill Prevention Control and Countermeasure Plan
Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA) Inc.
May 2014
http://www.ndhealth.gov/wm/publications/InvestigationsOfContaminantReleaseSi
tes.pdf
To prevent stormwater contamination, all impacted soils containing over 100 ppm of
TPH should be either removed to a State approved disposal site or removed to a secure
interim storage location for future remediation or disposal, unless more immediate on-site
remediation techniques can be implemented. (Placing the contaminated soil on a sheet of
visquene and covering it with a sheet of visquene is one acceptable method to prevent
stormwater contamination).
12.4.2 Spills onto Water
The source of the spill should be safely stopped and contained as soon as possible. If a
spill enters water, appropriate measures should be taken to remediate and contain the spill
through the use of floating booms or other mechanical barriers. Free oil may be removed
from the water by the use of a vacuum truck, oil-skimming equipment, and/or oil-
absorbent materials. Oil contaminated shoreline materials may require removal to a
suitable treatment site for cleanup, and may be cleaned as described in the Spills onto
Soil Section above.
Any spill that enters a watercourse or threatens to enter a watercourse, no matter the
quantity is required to be reported to the EPA National Response Center at (800) 424-
8802.
Oil spills onto surface waters of any quantity should be cleaned up to the satisfaction of
the landowner and regulatory agencies. Final standards for clean-up may be determined
on a case-by-case basis by the NDDOH. Action levels have been established for
petroleum compounds, and drinking water maximum contaminant levels may also be
used as action levels. NDDOH guidance for spills impacting groundwater or surface
water is found at :
http://www.ndhealth.gov/wm/publications/CleanupActionLevelsForGasolineAnd
OtherPetroleumHydrocarbons.pdf.
12.5
Emergency Response
In the event of an emergency, the following procedures will be followed. These
procedures are based on the U.S. Department of Transportation (DOT) guidelines for
such emergencies.
12.5.1 Fire
CAUTION: All these products have a very low flash point: Use of water spray when
fighting fire may be inefficient.
Small Fires

Dry chemical, CO2, water spray or regular foam.
27
Spill Prevention Control and Countermeasure Plan
Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA) Inc.
May 2014
Large Fires

Water spray, fog, or regular foam.

Use water spray or fog; do not use straight streams.

Move containers from fire area if you can do it without risk.
Fire Involving Tanks or Car/Trailer Loads

Fight fire from maximum distance or use unmanned hose holders or monitor
nozzles.

Cool containers with flooding quantities of water until well after fire is out.

Withdraw immediately in case of rising sound from venting safety devices or
discoloration of tank.

Always stay away from tanks engulfed in fire.

For massive fire, use unmanned hose holder or monitor nozzles; if this is
impossible, withdraw from area and let fire burn.
12.5.2 Spill or Leak
Small Spill

Eliminate all ignition sources (no smoking, flares, sparks or flames in immediate
area).

All equipment used when handling the product must be grounded.

Do not touch or walk through spilled material.

Stop leak if you can do it without risk.

Prevent entry into waterways, sewers, basements or confined areas.

A vapor suppressing foam may be used to reduce vapors.

Absorb or cover with dry earth, sand or other non-combustible material and
transfer to containers.

Use clean non-sparking tools to collect absorbed material.
Large Spill

Dike far ahead of liquid spill for later disposal.

Water spray may reduce vapor; but may not prevent ignition in closed spaces.
12.5.3 First Aid

Call 911 or emergency medical service.

Move victim to fresh air.

Apply artificial respiration if victim is not breathing.

Administer oxygen if breathing is difficult.

Remove and isolate contaminated clothing and shoes.

In case of contact with substance, immediately flush skin or eyes with running
water for at least 20 minutes.

Wash skin with soap and water.

Keep victim warm and quiet.

Ensure that medical personnel are aware of the material(s) involved, and take
precautions to protect themselves.

APPENDIX B
COMMITMENT OF MANPOWER EQUIPMENT
AND MATERIAL FOR SPILL CONTROL
Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA) Inc. (Kodiak) is committed to a strong antipollution and spill
prevention program. We are committed to designing and operating our facilities in a manner that
will minimize the size and occurrence of spills.
We are committed to a strong, pro-active
training and inspection program that should insure that our facilities are operated and maintained
in a manner that will prevent or minimize the occurrence of spills.
In the event of a spill, Kodiak will use whatever manpower, equipment and material that
will result in the spill being cleaned-up in an acceptable amount of time, with a minimum of
environmental damage and the maximum recovery of the spilled material practicable.
In accordance with the general and production facility specific portions of the SPCC rules,
secondary containment (in the form of berms) are provided for all bulk storage containers. This
equipment is noted in Section 2.1 and additionally is shown on the site specific plot plans in the
corresponding facility attachments.
Kodiak is dedicated to insuring that the secondary
containment for the bulk storage containers are constructed, maintained, and function as
designed.
Secondary containment or diversionary structures have been deemed impracticable for portions
of the facilities as noted in Section 2.3. In lieu of secondary containment for these areas, Kodiak
has prepared an Oil Spill Contingency Plan (found in Section 12.5) provided alternative
measures for secondary containment (outlined in Section 2.3) as well as making the following
provisions for spill response:
· Provided in Appendix G is a list of the local contractors, and their contact information
that Kodiak has developed agreements with for general and emergency site work. This
includes but is not limited to acquiring their services in the event of a discharge to stop
progression of a spill and remediation of any contamination caused by a spill.
· In the event of a discharge:
o
Operations personnel, holding safety paramount, will make all reasonable efforts
to stop further discharge once it is discovered.
o
When ever possible operations personnel will attempt to stop a discharge from
migrating off site or to surface waters.
When necessary, assistance from the
contractors listed in Appendix G may be used to stop progression of a spill.
o
When a spill is contained, spill reporting procedures as outlined in Appendix F
will be followed to notify management, as well as all applicable state, and federal
agencies.
o
Contractors listed in Appendix G will be used to aid in the remediation of any
contaminated areas following a discharge.
· Outlined in Section 12.5 are the materials made available for spill response and cleanup.

APPENDIX C
INSPECTION & EXAMINATION
PROCEDURES & GUIDANCE
Spill Prevention Control and Countermeasure Plan
Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA) Inc.
EXAMINATION &
INSPECTION PROGRAMS
The examination and inspection programs employed by Kodiak are generally aimed at
preventing discharges of oil caused by leak, brittle fracture and other forms of container failure.
As the facilities covered under this Plan are production facilities, they are exempt from the
mandatory integrity testing requirements of §112.8. However programs for examination and
inspection of bulk storage containers, pressure vessels, and flowlines have been developed for
these facilities to ensure that adequate environmental protection has been provided.
Detailed below are the examination and inspection programs that Kodiak currently employs:
Routine Operational Examinations:
Routine visual operational examinations of tanks, separators, piping, supports, foundations, and
saltwater injection equipment are conducted at each facility. These examinations are performed
by field operators during their routine on-site visits and are un-documented, unless an
abnormality is observed.
In the event that an issue is detected, the operator will take the
appropriate actions to prevent a discharge as described in Section 12.0 of this Plan. A summary
of the items addressed in these examinations has been provided in Appendix C.
Annual SPCC Inspections
At each site, a complete annual SPCC inspection will be performed in accordance with the
standards discussed in the SPCC Plan and will include as a minimum the items listed in the
SPCC Checklist. These inspections will be performed by a qualified inspector and documented
using the annual SPCC checklist (see Appendix C). If problems are identified, appropriate
corrective actions will be noted on the inspection form and prompt action will be taken for
repairs or replacement.
Equipment Examinations & Inspections*
Examinations and inspections relating to specific equipment will be performed as per the
standards stated in the SPCC Plan and their equipment specific guidance documents contained
within Appendix C. If problems are identified, appropriate corrective actions will be noted and
prompt action will be taken for repairs or replacement.
· External Condition Examination: Examinations - visual observations by field personnel
o
Unscheduled:
An external condition examination is typically made by the
operator when a routine operation examination reveals an abnormality.
o
Scheduled: As a part of the annual SPCC inspection, the external condition of
bulk storage containers, pressure vessels and all visible piping will be examined.
Spill Prevention Control and Countermeasure Plan
Kodiak Oil & Gas (USA) Inc.
· Internal Condition Examination: Examinations - visual observations by field personnel
o
Unscheduled:
In the event that a potential leak is identified, an internal
examination may be warranted if the vessel is taken out of service.
o
Scheduled: Internal examinations should be done whenever a piece of equipment
is cleaned, moved, when operational service is changed or during any internal
maintenance operation.
· Internal & External Inspections: Inspections ­ Appraisal of remaining corrosion life
o
Unscheduled:
If routine operational examinations reveal a leak or severe
corrosion, inspection may be warranted depending on the location of the flaw.
o
Scheduled:
Industry best practices recommend inspections at a minimum
whenever the minimum required plate thickness has been reached. A maximum
of 15 years is recommended for tanks and 10 years for pressure vessels.
*NOTE:
Industry best practices have been used as the basis of the examination/inspection
programs.
However, it should be noted that the detailed recordkeeping and measurement
comparison records requirements of §112.8 do not apply.
As such, Kodiak's associated
inspection practices vary slightly from the industry best practices.
Recordkeeping
Records of examinations and inspections are maintained at Kodiak's field and/or corporate
office. As the majority of the examinations are routine, visual and undocumented, the annual
inspections that accompany this Plan are the primary documentation associated with these
programs.
